<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2><A

Kraft Foods&nbsp;Inc. and Subsidiaries<BR>
Notes to Condensed Consolidated Financial Statements<BR>
(Unaudited) </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;1.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Accounting Policies:</U>  </B></FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Basis of Presentation  </I></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
interim condensed consolidated financial statements of Kraft Foods&nbsp;Inc. ("Kraft"), together with its subsidiaries (collectively referred to as the "Company"), are unaudited. It is the
opinion of the Company's management that all adjustments necessary for a fair statement of the interim results presented have been reflected therein. All such adjustments were of a normal recurring
nature. Net revenues and net earnings for any interim period are not necessarily indicative of results that may be expected for the entire year. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>These
statements should be read in conjunction with the Company's consolidated financial statements and related notes, and management's discussion and analysis of financial condition and results of
operations, which appear in the Company's Annual Report to Shareholders and which are incorporated by reference into the Company's Annual Report on Form&nbsp;10-K for the year ended
December&nbsp;31, 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Certain
prior year amounts have been reclassified to conform with the current year's presentation, due primarily to the disclosure of more detailed information on the condensed consolidated balance
sheets, the condensed consolidated statements of earnings and the condensed consolidated statements of cash flows, as well as the transfer of Canadian Biscuits and Pet Snacks operations from the
Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery segment to the Cheese, Meals and Enhancers segment, which contains the Company's other Canadian businesses. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Stock-Based Compensation Expense  </I></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company accounts for employee stock compensation plans in accordance with the intrinsic value-based method permitted by Statement of Financial Accounting Standards ("SFAS") No.&nbsp;123,
"Accounting for Stock-Based Compensation," which did not result in compensation cost for stock options. The market value of restricted stock at date of grant is recorded as compensation expense over
the period of restriction. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first quarter of 2003, the Company granted shares of restricted stock and rights to receive shares of stock under its 2001 Performance Incentive Plan to eligible employees, giving them in
most instances all of the rights of shareholders, except that they may not sell, assign, pledge or otherwise encumber such shares and rights. Such shares and rights are subject to forfeiture if
certain employment conditions are not met. The shares of restricted stock granted comprised approximately 3.7&nbsp;million Class&nbsp;A shares to eligible U.S.-based employees and rights to
receive approximately 1.6&nbsp;million Class&nbsp;A equivalent shares to eligible non-U.S. employees. Restrictions on the stock lapse in the first quarter of 2006. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
fair value of the restricted shares and rights at the date of grant is amortized to expense ratably over the restriction period. The Company recorded compensation expense related to restricted
stock and other stock awards of $43&nbsp;million and $16&nbsp;million, respectively, for the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003. The unamortized portion, which is
reported on the condensed consolidated balance sheets as a reduction of earnings reinvested in the business, was $146&nbsp;million at September&nbsp;30, 2003. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>In
addition to restricted stock, at September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company had stock-based employee compensation plans that permit the issuance of stock options to employees. The Company applies the
recognition and measurement principles of Accounting Principles Board Opinion No.&nbsp;25, "Accounting for Stock Issued to Employees," and related Interpretations in accounting for those plans. No
compensation expense for employee stock options is reflected in net earnings as all options granted under those plans had an exercise price equal to the market value of the common stock on the date of
the grant. Net earnings, as reported, includes the after-tax impact of compensation expense related to restricted stock. The following tables illustrate the effect on net earnings and
earnings per share ("EPS") if the Company had applied the fair value recognition provisions of </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>10</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;3.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Related Party Transactions:</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>At
September&nbsp;30, 2003, Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. owned approximately 84.2% of the Company's outstanding shares of capital stock. Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc.'s subsidiary, Altria Corporate
Services,&nbsp;Inc., provides the Company with various services, including planning, legal, treasury, accounting, auditing, insurance, human resources, office of the secretary, corporate affairs,
information technology and tax services. Billings for these services, which were based on the cost to Altria Corporate Services,&nbsp;Inc. to provide such services and a management fee, were
$243&nbsp;million and $227&nbsp;million for the nine months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003 and 2002, respectively, and $78&nbsp;million and $69&nbsp;million for the three months ended
September&nbsp;30, 2003 and 2002, respectively. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Notes
payable to Altria Group, Inc. and affiliates consisted of the following: </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During the first quarter of 2003, the Company repaid the remaining $1.15&nbsp;billion to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. on the 7.0% note. In September&nbsp;2003, the Company

10-year notes bearing interest at a rate of 5.25%. The net proceeds from the offering were used to refinance a portion of the short-term notes payable to Altria
Group,&nbsp;Inc. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company had short-term debt totaling $181&nbsp;million </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;4.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Acquisitions and Divestitures:</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the third quarter of 2003, the Company acquired trademarks associated with a small U.S.-based natural foods business. In the second quarter of 2003, the Company acquired a biscuits business in
Egypt. During the third quarter of 2002, the Company acquired a snacks business in Turkey and, during the first quarter of 2002, the Company acquired a biscuits business in Australia. The total cost
of these and other smaller businesses purchased during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 was $97&nbsp;million and $119&nbsp;million, respectively. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the third quarter of 2003, the Company sold a European rice business. During the first half of 2002, the Company sold several small North American food businesses, most of which were previously
classified as businesses held for sale. The aggregate pre-tax proceeds received from sales of businesses during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 were $25&nbsp;million and
$84&nbsp;million, respectively. The Company recorded pre-tax gains from sales of businesses during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 of $23&nbsp;million and $3&nbsp;million,
respectively. The net revenues and operating results of the businesses held for sale, which were not significant, were excluded from the Company's condensed consolidated statements of earnings and no
gain or loss was recognized on these sales. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>In
September&nbsp;2003, the Company announced an agreement to sell a branded fresh cheese business in Italy. The transaction is subject to approval by the Italian competition authorities. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
operating results of businesses acquired and divested were not material to the Company's consolidated financial position, results of operations or cash flows in any of the periods presented. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;6.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Contingencies:</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company and its subsidiaries are parties to a variety of legal proceedings arising out of the normal course of business, including a few cases in which substantial amounts of damages are sought.
While the results of litigation cannot be predicted with certainty, management believes that the final outcome of these proceedings will not have a material adverse effect on the Company's
consolidated financial position, results of operations or cash flows. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Guarantees:</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;At September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company's third-party guarantees, which are primarily derived from acquisition and
divestiture activities, approximated $35&nbsp;million. Substantially all of these guarantees expire through 2013, with $22&nbsp;million expiring through September&nbsp;30, 2004. The Company is
required to perform under these guarantees in the event that a third party fails to make contractual payments or achieve performance measures. The Company has recorded a liability of
$25&nbsp;million at September&nbsp;30, 2003 relating to these guarantees. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Non-amortizable intangible assets consist substantially of brand names purchased through the Nabisco acquisition. Amortizable intangible assets consist primarily of
certain trademark licenses and non-compete agreements. Pre-tax amortization expense for intangible assets was $7&nbsp;million for the nine months ended September&nbsp;30,
2003 and $2&nbsp;million for the three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003. Based upon the amortizable intangible assets recorded in the condensed consolidated balance sheet at
September&nbsp;30, 2003, amortization expense for each of the next five years is estimated to be $10&nbsp;million or less. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;8.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Segment Reporting:</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company manufactures and markets packaged retail food products, consisting principally of beverages, cheese, snacks, convenient meals and various packaged grocery products through Kraft Foods
North America,&nbsp;Inc. ("KFNA") and Kraft Foods International,&nbsp;Inc. ("KFI"). Reportable segments for KFNA are organized and managed principally by product category. KFNA's segments are
Cheese, Meals and Enhancers; Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery; Beverages, Desserts and Cereals; and Oscar Mayer and Pizza. KFNA's food service business within the United States and its businesses in
Canada and Mexico are reported through the Cheese, Meals and Enhancers segment. KFI's operations are organized and managed by geographic location. KFI's segments are Europe, Middle East and Africa;
and Latin America and Asia Pacific. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company's management uses operating companies income, which is defined as operating income before general corporate expenses and amortization of intangibles, to evaluate segment performance and
allocate resources. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Interest
and other debt expense, net, and provision for income taxes are centrally managed and, accordingly, such items are not presented by segment since they are not included in the measure of
segment profitability reviewed by management. </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>15</FONT></P>

the first quarter of 2003, the Company transferred management responsibility of its Canadian Biscuits and Pet Snacks operations from the Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery segment to the
Cheese, Meals and Enhancers segment, which contains the Company's other Canadian businesses. Accordingly, all prior period amounts have been reclassified to reflect the transfer. </FONT></P>

segment data were as follows: </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Note&nbsp;9.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Financial Instruments:</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003, ineffectiveness related to cash flow hedges was a gain of $13&nbsp;million, which was recorded in cost of sales on the
condensed consolidated statements of earnings. Ineffectiveness related to cash flow hedges during the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2002 was not material. At
September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company was hedging forecasted transactions for periods not exceeding the next fourteen months and expects substantially all amounts reported in accumulated other
comprehensive earnings (losses) to be reclassified to the consolidated statement of earnings within the next twelve months. </FONT></P>

<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="72%" ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2><B>MANAGEMENT'S DISCUSSION AND ANALYSIS OF</B></FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="12%"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>

<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="72%" ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2><B>FINANCIAL CONDITION AND RESULTS OF OPERATIONS</B></FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="12%" ALIGN="RIGHT"><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
</TR>
</TABLE>
<!-- end of user-specified TAGGED TABLE -->


<P><FONT SIZE=2><B>Business Environment  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Kraft
Foods&nbsp;Inc. ("Kraft"), together with its subsidiaries (collectively referred to as the "Company"), is the largest branded food and beverage company headquartered in the United States. At
September&nbsp;30, 2003, Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. held 97.8% of the combined voting power of Kraft's outstanding capital stock and owned approximately 84.2% of the outstanding shares of Kraft's
capital stock. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company conducts its global business through two subsidiaries: Kraft Foods North America,&nbsp;Inc. ("KFNA") and Kraft Foods International,&nbsp;Inc. ("KFI"). KFNA manages its operations
principally by product category, while KFI manages its operations by geographic region. KFNA's segments are Cheese, Meals and Enhancers; Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery; Beverages, Desserts and
Cereals; and Oscar Mayer and Pizza. KFNA's food service business within the United States and its businesses in Canada and Mexico are reported through the Cheese, Meals and Enhancers segment. KFI's
segments are Europe, Middle East and Africa; and Latin America and Asia Pacific. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company is subject to a number of challenges that may adversely affect its businesses. These challenges, which are discussed below and under the "Forward-Looking and Cautionary Statements,"
include: </FONT></P>

<UL>
<DL compact>
<DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>fluctuations
in commodity prices;
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>movements
of foreign currencies against the U.S. dollar;
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>competitive
challenges in various products and markets, including price gaps with competitor products;
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>a
trend toward increasing consolidation in the retail trade and consequent inventory reductions;
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>changing
consumer preferences;
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>competitors
with different profit objectives and less susceptibility to currency exchange rates; and
<BR><BR></FONT></DD><DT style='margin-bottom:-11pt;'><FONT SIZE=2>&#149;</FONT></DT><DD><FONT SIZE=2>consumer
concerns about food safety, quality and health, including concerns about genetically modified organisms, trans fatty acids and obesity. </FONT></DD></DL>
</UL>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>To
confront these challenges, the Company continues to take steps to build the value of its brands, to improve its food business portfolio with new product and marketing initiatives, to reduce costs
through productivity and to address consumer concerns about food safety, quality and health, including obesity. In July&nbsp;2003, the Company announced a range of initiatives addressing product
nutrition, marketing practices, consumer information and public advocacy and dialogue. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the second quarter of 2003, several factors contributed to lower than anticipated volume growth. These factors included trade inventory reductions, resulting from several customers experiencing
financial difficulty, warehouse consolidations, store closings and retailers' stated initiatives to reduce working capital, as well as the combined adverse effect of global economic weakness and
higher price gaps in some key categories and countries. To improve volume and share trends, the Company announced that it would increase spending behind certain U.S. businesses in the balance of 2003.
The incremental investment is expected to be approximately $200&nbsp;million higher than previously planned amounts, of which approximately $45&nbsp;million has been spent </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>20</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=1,SEQ=20,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=666425,FOLIO='20',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DQ4659A.;8',USER='MWEINST',CD='13-NOV-2003;09:56' -->
<A NAME="page_dq4659_1_21"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>through
September&nbsp;30, 2003. The Company also anticipates increased spending behind certain U.S. businesses in 2004. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Fluctuations
in commodity prices can lead to retail price volatility and intensive price competition, and can influence consumer and trade buying patterns. KFNA's and KFI's businesses are subject to
fluctuating commodity costs, including dairy, coffee and cocoa costs. In 2003, the Company's commodity costs on average have been higher than those incurred in 2002. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>On
December&nbsp;11, 2000, the Company acquired all of the outstanding shares of Nabisco Holdings Corp. ("Nabisco"). The closure of a number of Nabisco domestic and international facilities resulted
in severance and other exit costs of $379&nbsp;million, which were included in the adjustments for the allocation of the Nabisco purchase price. The closures will require total cash payments of
$373&nbsp;million, of which approximately $220&nbsp;million has been spent through September&nbsp;30, 2003. Substantially all closures are completed and the remaining payments relate to salary
continuation payments for severed employees and lease payments. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
integration of Nabisco into the operations of the Company has also resulted in the closure or reconfiguration of several of the Company's existing facilities. During the first quarter of 2002,
approximately 700 salaried employees elected to retire or terminate employment under voluntary retirement programs. As a result, the Company recorded a pre-tax charge of
$142&nbsp;million during the first quarter of 2002 related to these programs. In addition, during the first quarter of 2002, the Company recorded pre-tax integration related charges of
$27&nbsp;million to consolidate production lines in North America. During the second quarter of 2002, the Company recorded pre-tax integration related charges of $92&nbsp;million for
the closing of a facility and other integration programs. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the third quarter of 2003, the Company recorded a pre-tax charge of $6&nbsp;million for asset impairment and exit costs related to the closure of a Nordic snacks plant. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the third quarter of 2003, the Company acquired trademarks associated with a small U.S.-based natural foods business. In the second quarter of 2003, the Company acquired a biscuits business in
Egypt. During the third quarter of 2002, the Company acquired a snacks business in Turkey and, during the first quarter of 2002, the Company acquired a biscuits business in Australia. The total cost
of these and other smaller businesses purchased during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 was $97&nbsp;million and $119&nbsp;million, respectively. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the third quarter of 2003, the Company sold a European rice business. During the first half of 2002, the Company sold several small North American food businesses, most of which were previously
classified as businesses held for sale. The aggregate pre-tax proceeds received from sales of businesses during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 were $25&nbsp;million and
$84&nbsp;million, respectively. The Company recorded pre-tax gains from sales of businesses during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 of $23&nbsp;million and $3&nbsp;million,
respectively. The net revenues and operating results of the businesses held for sale, which were not significant, were excluded from the Company's condensed consolidated statements of earnings and no
gain or loss was recognized on these sales. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>In
September&nbsp;2003, the Company announced an agreement to sell a branded fresh cheese business in Italy. The transaction is subject to approval by the Italian competition authorities. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
operating results of businesses acquired and divested were not material to the Company's consolidated financial position, results of operations or cash flows in any of the periods presented. </FONT></P>

following events occurred during the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 that affected the comparability of statement of earnings amounts: </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>As discussed in Note&nbsp;8. </FONT><FONT SIZE=2><I>Segment Reporting</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>, management reviews operating companies income, which is defined as operating
income before general corporate expenses and amortization of intangibles, to evaluate segment performance and allocate resources. Management believes it is appropriate to disclose this measure to help
investors analyze business performances and trends of the various business segments. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Consolidated Results of Operations for the Nine Months Ended September&nbsp;30, 2003  </I></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discussion compares consolidated operating results for the nine months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003 with the nine months ended September&nbsp;30, 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume
increased 0.6%, due primarily to increased shipments in the Beverages, Desserts and Cereals segment, growth in developing markets, new product introductions and the impact of acquisitions,
partially offset by the impact of divested businesses, lower consumption in certain categories, particularly U.S. cookies, and the impact of a weaker global economy. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $804&nbsp;million (3.7%), due to favorable currency ($440&nbsp;million), higher pricing ($315&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven
costs, partially offset by higher promotional spending), higher volume/mix ($86&nbsp;million) and the impact of acquisitions ($44&nbsp;million), partially offset by the impact of divested
businesses ($81&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
income increased $43&nbsp;million (1.0%), due primarily to the impact of pre-tax charges for asset impairment and exit costs in 2003 and 2002 ($136&nbsp;million), integration
costs in 2002 ($119&nbsp;million) and favorable currency ($50&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher fixed manufacturing costs ($132&nbsp;million, including higher benefit costs), higher
marketing, administration and research costs ($77&nbsp;million), unfavorable volume/mix ($46&nbsp;million) and unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($9&nbsp;million, due primarily to higher
commodity costs and increased promotional spending). The higher benefit costs were primarily related to pension and stock compensation costs. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Currency
movements increased net revenues by $440&nbsp;million and operating income by $50&nbsp;million. These increases are due primarily to the weakness of the U.S. dollar against the euro, the
Canadian dollar and other currencies, partially offset by the strength of the U.S. dollar against certain Latin American currencies. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Interest
and other debt expense, net, decreased $159&nbsp;million. This decrease is due to the Company's refinancing of notes payable to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc., lower short-term
interest rates and the use of free cash flow to pay down debt. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
earnings of $2,607&nbsp;million increased $144&nbsp;million (5.8%), due primarily to lower interest expense and growth in operating income. Diluted and basic earnings per share ("EPS"), which
were both $1.51, increased by 6.3%. </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>24</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=3,SEQ=24,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=602020,FOLIO='24',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DS4659A.;14',USER='MBLOUNT',CD='13-NOV-2003;12:26' -->
<A NAME="page_ds4659_1_25"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Consolidated Results of Operations for the Three Months Ended September&nbsp;30, 2003  </I></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discussion compares consolidated operating results for the third quarter of 2003 with the third quarter of 2002. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume
decreased 0.2%, due primarily to the impact of divested businesses and lower consumption in certain categories, particularly U.S. cookies and European chocolate and coffee, which were adversely
affected by the summer heat wave across Europe. These declines were partially offset by the impact of acquisitions and increased shipments in several North American businesses and across the Asia
Pacific region. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $264&nbsp;million (3.7%), due to favorable currency ($213&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($102&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven
costs, partially offset by higher promotional spending) and the impact of acquisitions ($23&nbsp;million), partially offset by lower volume/mix ($42&nbsp;million) and the impact of divested
businesses ($32&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
income decreased $150&nbsp;million (9.6%), due primarily to unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($75&nbsp;million due primarily to higher commodity costs and increased promotional
spending), higher fixed manufacturing costs ($48&nbsp;million, including higher benefit costs) and lower volume/mix ($43&nbsp;million), partially offset by favorable currency ($27&nbsp;million)
and the gain on the sale of a rice business in Europe ($23&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Currency
movements increased net revenues by $213&nbsp;million and operating income by $27&nbsp;million. These increases in net revenues and operating income are due primarily to the weakness of
the U.S. dollar against the euro, the Canadian dollar and other currencies, partially offset by the strength of the U.S. dollar against certain Latin American currencies. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Interest
and other debt expense, net, decreased $51&nbsp;million. This decrease is due to the Company's refinancing of notes payable to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc., lower short-term
interest rates and the use of free cash flow to pay down debt. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
earnings of $810&nbsp;million decreased $59&nbsp;million (6.8%), due primarily to lower operating income, partially offset by lower interest expense. Diluted and basic EPS, which were both
$0.47, decreased by 6.0%. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first quarter of 2003, the Company transferred management responsibility of its Canadian Biscuits and Pet Snacks operations from the Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery segment to the
Cheese, Meals and Enhancers segment, which contains the Company's other Canadian businesses. Accordingly, all prior period amounts have been reclassified to reflect the transfer. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume
increased 1.6%, due primarily to increased shipments in the Beverages, Desserts and Cereals segment, partially offset by the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002 and
consumption weakness in certain categories, primarily cookies. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $263&nbsp;million (1.6%), due primarily to higher volume/mix ($154&nbsp;million), higher pricing, net of increased promotional spending ($67&nbsp;million) and favorable
currency ($58&nbsp;million), partially offset by the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002 ($17&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $62&nbsp;million (1.6%), due primarily to the 2002 pre-tax charges for asset impairment and exit costs ($135&nbsp;million) and integration costs
($102&nbsp;million) and lower marketing, administration and research costs ($20&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher fixed manufacturing costs ($98&nbsp;million, including higher benefit
costs), unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($90&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased promotional spending) and unfavorable volume/mix ($13&nbsp;million). As discussed
above in the Business Environment section, the Company increased spending behind certain U.S. businesses during the third quarter of 2003. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discusses operating results within each of KFNA's reportable segments. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Cheese, Meals and Enhancers</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 1.3%, due primarily to higher shipments in Canada, food service and Mexico, partially offset
by reduced trade inventories in certain cheese and enhancers businesses. Volume in Canada and Mexico increased, driven by new beverage product introductions. Volume for the food service business in
the United States also increased, due to higher shipments of beverages, cheese, salad dressings and meat to national accounts and increased demand for ingredient products. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $97&nbsp;million (1.4%), due to favorable currency ($58&nbsp;million), higher volume/mix ($24&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($15&nbsp;million, including the impact of
increased promotional spending). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $4&nbsp;million (0.2%), due primarily to the 2002 pre-tax charges for asset impairment and exit costs ($60&nbsp;million) and integration costs
($35&nbsp;million) and favorable currency ($10&nbsp;million), partially offset by unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($45&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased
promotional spending), unfavorable volume/mix ($23&nbsp;million), higher fixed manufacturing costs ($21&nbsp;million) and higher marketing, administration and research costs ($13&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>26</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=5,SEQ=26,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=579953,FOLIO='26',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DS4659A.;14',USER='MBLOUNT',CD='13-NOV-2003;12:26' -->
<A NAME="page_ds4659_1_27"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume decreased 3.9%, due primarily to consumption weakness in cookies and the divestiture of a small
confectionery business in 2002, partially offset by higher shipments in snacks, which benefited from category and consumption gains in snack nuts. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues decreased $3&nbsp;million (0.1%), due to the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002 ($17&nbsp;million) and higher promotional spending, net of higher pricing
($6&nbsp;million), partially offset by favorable volume/mix ($20&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $63&nbsp;million (8.3%), due primarily to higher fixed manufacturing costs ($73&nbsp;million), lower volume/mix ($31&nbsp;million), and unfavorable costs,
net of higher pricing ($29&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased promotional spending), partially offset by lower marketing, administration and research costs
($70&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Beverages, Desserts and Cereals</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 5.4%, due primarily to higher shipments of ready-to-drink
beverages, which were aided by new product introductions. Desserts volume also increased, due primarily to higher shipments of refrigerated ready-to-eat desserts, shelf stable
desserts and nutrition/energy bars. In coffee, volume declined due primarily to higher prices, which resulted in category and share declines. Cereals volume also declined due to category and share
declines in ready-to-eat cereal. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $105&nbsp;million (3.1%), due to higher volume/mix ($69&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($36&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $117&nbsp;million (13.2%), due primarily to the 2002 pre-tax charges for integration costs ($59&nbsp;million) and asset impairment and exit costs
($47&nbsp;million) and higher volume/mix ($26&nbsp;million), partially offset by unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($9&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs). </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Oscar Mayer and Pizza</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 0.9%, due primarily to gains in frozen pizza, bacon, lunch combinations and soy-based
meat alternatives, partially offset by lower consumption of cold cuts. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $64&nbsp;million (2.8%), due primarily to higher volume/mix ($41&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($22&nbsp;million, including the impact of increased promotional
spending). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $4&nbsp;million (0.9%), due primarily to the 2002 pre-tax charges for asset impairment and exit costs ($25&nbsp;million) and integration costs
($7&nbsp;million) and higher volume/mix ($15&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher marketing, administration and research costs ($37&nbsp;million) and unfavorable costs, net of higher
pricing ($7&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased promotional spending). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume decreased 2.4%, due to the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002 and a rice business in Europe in 2003, partially offset by growth in
developing markets, new product introductions and the acquisitions of a snacks business in Turkey and a biscuits business in Egypt. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $541&nbsp;million (9.3%), due to favorable currency ($382&nbsp;million), higher pricing ($248&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven
costs in Latin America) and the impact of acquisitions ($43&nbsp;million), partially offset by lower volume/mix ($68&nbsp;million) and the impact of divestitures ($64&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $11&nbsp;million (1.3%), due primarily to higher marketing, administration and research costs ($97&nbsp;million), lower volume/mix ($33&nbsp;million) and the
impact of divestitures ($12&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher pricing, net of cost increases ($47&nbsp;million, including fixed manufacturing costs), favorable currency
($40&nbsp;million), gains on sales of businesses ($20&nbsp;million), the 2002 pre-tax charges for integration costs ($17&nbsp;million) and the impact of acquisitions
($6&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discusses operating results within each of KFI's reportable segments. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Europe, Middle East and Africa</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 0.4%, due to volume growth across the Central and Eastern Europe, Middle East and Africa
region, which benefited from the recent acquisition of a biscuits business in Egypt as well as a snacks business acquisition in Turkey during the third quarter of 2002, and new product introductions.
These gains were partially offset by the impact of the summer heat wave across Europe on the chocolate and coffee businesses, price competition and the divestiture of a rice business in Europe in
2003. Snacks volume increased, benefiting from acquisitions and the growth of confectionery businesses in Russia, Poland and Romania, partially offset by lower volume due to the summer heat wave
across Europe and price competition. In beverages, volume declined, due primarily to the summer heat wave across Europe (which had an adverse impact on coffee shipments), price competition and trade
inventory reductions, partially offset by increased coffee shipments in Russia and Poland. Convenient meals volume declined due to the divestiture of a rice business in Europe, partially offset by
higher shipments of canned meats in Italy. Cheese </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>28</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=1,SEQ=28,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=545655,FOLIO='28',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DU4659A.;9',USER='MBLOUNT',CD='13-NOV-2003;12:31' -->
<A NAME="page_du4659_1_29"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>volume
also declined, due primarily to the impact of price competition in Germany and Spain and lower shipments in the Middle East, partially offset by gains in cream cheese in Italy. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $615&nbsp;million (14.4%), due to favorable currency ($616&nbsp;million), the impact of acquisitions ($43&nbsp;million) and higher pricing, partially offset by unfavorable
volume/mix ($46&nbsp;million) and the 2003 divestiture of a rice business in Europe. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $44&nbsp;million (7.1%), due primarily to favorable currency ($72&nbsp;million) and the gain on the sale of a rice business in Europe ($23&nbsp;million),
partially offset by unfavorable volume/mix ($23&nbsp;million), unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($15&nbsp;million) and higher marketing, administration and research costs
($15&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Latin America and Asia Pacific</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume decreased 6.3%, due to the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002,
political and economic instability in Venezuela and economic weakness in Brazil, partially offset by growth in Argentina and across most Asia Pacific markets. Snacks volume decreased, due primarily to
political and economic instability in Venezuela, and a confectionery market decline in Brazil, partially offset by biscuits volume growth in many markets, including Brazil, Colombia, Central America,
China, Australia and Southeast Asia. In grocery, volume declined in Latin America, due primarily to the sale of a bakery ingredients business in the fourth quarter of 2002. In beverages, volume grew
in both coffee and refreshment beverages. Coffee volume increased, due primarily to higher shipments to China. Refreshment beverages volume increased, due to growth in most markets, including Brazil,
Venezuela, China and the Philippines, aided by new product introductions. In cheese, volume increased, due to higher shipments to Japan, the Philippines and Australia, partially offset by declines in
Venezuela and Indonesia. Convenient meals volume also grew, benefiting from gains in Argentina. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues decreased $74&nbsp;million (4.9%), due to unfavorable currency ($234&nbsp;million), the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002 ($58&nbsp;million) and
lower volume/mix ($22&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher pricing ($240&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven costs). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $55&nbsp;million (24.0%), due primarily to higher marketing, administration and research costs ($82&nbsp;million), unfavorable currency ($32&nbsp;million),
lower volume/mix ($10&nbsp;million) and the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002 ($9&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher pricing, net of cost increases
($62&nbsp;million) and the 2002 pre-tax charges for integration costs ($17&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume increased 0.9%, due primarily to increased shipments in ready-to-drink beverages, food service, enhancers and meats, partially offset by lower
consumption in certain categories, particularly cookies, and the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $101&nbsp;million (1.9%), due primarily to favorable currency ($46&nbsp;million), higher volume/mix ($36&nbsp;million) and higher pricing, partially offset by higher
promotional spending, including investments to manage price gaps and improve share trends in cheese, cold cuts, coffee and biscuits, and the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $151&nbsp;million (11.6%), due primarily to cost increases, net of higher pricing (aggregating $112&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and
increased promotional spending in certain categories), higher fixed manufacturing costs ($42&nbsp;million, including higher benefit costs) and unfavorable volume/mix ($14&nbsp;million), partially
offset by favorable currency ($9&nbsp;million). As discussed above in the Business Environment section, the Company increased spending behind certain U.S. businesses during the third quarter of
2003. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discusses operating results within each of KFNA's reportable segments. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Cheese, Meals and Enhancers</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 1.5%, due primarily to volume gains in food service, enhancers, meals and Mexico. Volume in
the food service business in the United States increased, due to higher shipments to national accounts. In meals and enhancers, volume increased due to increased merchandising, primarily in dinners,
salad dressings and barbecue sauce. Volume in Mexico increased, aided by new ready-to-drink beverage products. Cheese volume was comparable with the prior year as improving
consumption trends were offset by trade inventory reductions. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $80&nbsp;million (3.7%), due to favorable currency ($46&nbsp;million), higher volume/mix ($19&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($15&nbsp;million, net of increased
promotional spending). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $65&nbsp;million (11.5%), due primarily to unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($68&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased
promotional spending in cheese to improve share trends) and higher fixed manufacturing costs ($9&nbsp;million), partially offset by favorable currency ($9&nbsp;million) and higher volume/mix. </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>30</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=3,SEQ=30,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=178453,FOLIO='30',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DU4659A.;9',USER='MBLOUNT',CD='13-NOV-2003;12:31' -->
<A NAME="page_du4659_1_31"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Biscuits, Snacks and Confectionery</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume decreased 3.9%, due primarily to lower shipments in biscuits, primarily cookies, and the
divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002. In biscuits, volume declined, due to reduced consumption in cookies, reflecting higher pricing, lower impact of new products and consumer
switching to alternate categories, partially offset by gains in crackers, which was aided by a new product introduction. In snacks, volume increased as the nuts category benefited from growing
consumer recognition of nuts' health benefits. In confectionery, volume also increased, led by higher shipments in the non-chocolate candy business. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues decreased $27&nbsp;million (2.2%), due to lower volume/mix ($16&nbsp;million), the divestiture of a small confectionery business in 2002 ($7&nbsp;million) and increased promotional
spending, net of higher pricing. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $55&nbsp;million (19.3%), due primarily to lower volume/mix ($34&nbsp;million), higher fixed manufacturing costs ($27&nbsp;million) and cost increases, net
of higher pricing (aggregating $22&nbsp;million, driven by higher commodity costs and increased promotional spending in biscuits), partially offset by lower marketing, administration and research
costs ($29&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Beverages, Desserts and Cereals</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 2.4%, due primarily to higher shipments of ready-to-drink
beverages, which were aided by new product introductions, and higher shipments of powdered soft drinks. Volume also increased in desserts, led by growth in shelf stable desserts behind increased
merchandising programs, as well as refrigerated ready-to-eat desserts and nutrition/energy bars, both of which benefited from new product introductions. In cereals, volume was
down due to category and share declines in ready-to-eat cereal. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $21&nbsp;million (2.0%), due to higher volume/mix ($15&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($6&nbsp;million, net of increased promotional spending). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $15&nbsp;million (5.2%), due primarily to higher marketing, administration and research costs ($10&nbsp;million), unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing
($6&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased promotional spending) and higher fixed manufacturing costs ($5&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher volume/mix
($7&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Oscar Mayer and Pizza</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>.&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume increased 1.8%, due primarily to volume gains in cold cuts, hot dogs, bacon, lunch combinations and
soy-based meat alternatives, partially offset by a decline in frozen pizza. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $27&nbsp;million (3.5%), due primarily to higher volume/mix ($18&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($8&nbsp;million, including commodity-driven price increases in meats,
partially offset by increased promotional spending in cold cuts to improve share trends). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income decreased $16&nbsp;million (9.7%), due primarily to unfavorable costs, net of higher pricing ($16&nbsp;million, including higher commodity costs and increased
promotional spending) and higher marketing, administration and research costs ($6&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher volume/mix ($9&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><A
NAME="page_dw4659_1_32"> </A> </FONT> <FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Kraft Foods International,&nbsp;Inc.</U>  </B></FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2><B>Operating Results  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discussion compares KFI's operating results for the third quarter of 2003 with the third quarter of 2002. </FONT></P>

<!-- User-specified TAGGED TABLE -->
<TABLE WIDTH="100%" BORDER=0 CELLSPACING=0 CELLPADDING=0>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Volume
decreased 3.2%, due to the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002 and a European rice business in 2003, the adverse impact of a summer heat wave across Europe on
the chocolate and coffee businesses, and price competition, partially offset by the acquisitions of a snacks business in Turkey and a biscuits business in Egypt and gains across all key markets in
Asia Pacific. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $163&nbsp;million (8.2%), due to favorable currency ($167&nbsp;million), higher pricing ($77&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven cost
increases in Latin America) and the impact of acquisitions ($22&nbsp;million), partially offset by lower volume/mix ($78&nbsp;million) and the impact of divestitures ($25&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $7&nbsp;million (2.3%), due primarily to higher pricing, net of cost increases ($31&nbsp;million, including fixed manufacturing costs), the gain on the sale of
a rice business in Europe ($23&nbsp;million) and favorable currency ($18&nbsp;million), partially offset by lower volume/mix ($29&nbsp;million), higher marketing, administration and research
costs ($27&nbsp;million, including higher benefit costs and infrastructure investment in developing markets) and the impact of divestitures ($6&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
following discusses operating results within each of KFI's reportable segments. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Europe, Middle East and Africa.</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume decreased 1.4%, driven by the declines in European chocolate and coffee and the impact of divested
businesses, partially offset by the impact of acquisitions and growth in Poland, Russia, Romania and Ukraine. Beverages volume decreased, driven by the adverse impact of the summer heat wave across
Europe on coffee, and price competition in Germany, partially offset by expanded distribution of coffee in Russia and new product introductions in Poland. In convenient meals, volume declined due
primarily to the divestiture of the rice business in Europe, partially offset by higher shipments of canned meats in Italy. Snacks volume increased, with gains in biscuits and salted snacks,
benefiting from acquisitions, partially offset by the adverse impact of the summer heat wave across Europe on chocolate shipments. </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>32</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=1,SEQ=32,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=480969,FOLIO='32',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DW4659A.;16',USER='DINGRAM',CD='12-NOV-2003;18:19' -->
<A NAME="page_dw4659_1_33"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $142&nbsp;million (9.5%), due to favorable currency ($175&nbsp;million), the impact of acquisitions ($22&nbsp;million) and higher pricing ($14&nbsp;million), partially
offset by lower volume/mix ($63&nbsp;million) and the divestiture of a rice business in Europe ($6&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $3&nbsp;million (1.3%), due primarily to the gain on the sale of a rice business in Europe ($23&nbsp;million), favorable currency ($22&nbsp;million) and
higher pricing, net of cost increases ($7&nbsp;million), partially offset by lower volume/mix ($25&nbsp;million), higher marketing, administration and research costs ($18&nbsp;million, including
higher benefit costs and infrastructure investment in eastern Europe) and the 2003 pre-tax charge for asset impairment and exit costs associated with the announced closure of a Nordic
snacks plant ($6&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Latin America and Asia Pacific.</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Volume decreased 5.5%, due to the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002,
partially offset by gains across all key markets in Asia Pacific. Snacks volume declined, due primarily to economic weakness and trade inventory reductions in Brazil and raw material unavailability in
Venezuela, partially offset by new product launches in Argentina, Australia and China. In grocery, volume declined, due primarily to the sale of the Latin American bakery ingredients business in the
fourth quarter of 2002. In beverages, volume increased, driven by gains in Brazil, Venezuela and China. Cheese volume also increased, due primarily to higher shipments in Australia and the
Philippines. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Net
revenues increased $21&nbsp;million (4.3%), due to higher pricing ($63&nbsp;million, reflecting higher commodity and currency devaluation-driven costs), partially offset by the divestiture of
a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002 ($19&nbsp;million), lower volume/mix ($15&nbsp;million) and unfavorable currency ($8&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Operating
companies income increased $4&nbsp;million (6.2%), due to higher pricing, net of cost increases ($24&nbsp;million), partially offset by higher marketing, administration and research
costs ($9&nbsp;million), unfavorable currency ($4&nbsp;million), lower volume/mix ($4&nbsp;million) and the divestiture of a Latin American bakery ingredients business in 2002
($3&nbsp;million). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Financial Review</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><U>Net
Cash Provided by Operating Activities</U> </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first nine months of 2003, net cash provided by operating activities was $2.8&nbsp;billion compared with $2.2&nbsp;billion in the comparable 2002 period. The increase in net cash
provided by operating activities is due
primarily to a lower use of cash to satisfy amounts due to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc., and a lower level of cash used to fund working capital. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><U>Net
Cash Used in Investing Activities</U> </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>One
element of the growth strategy of the Company is to strengthen its brand portfolios through disciplined programs of selective acquisitions and divestitures. The Company is constantly investigating
potential acquisition candidates and from time to time sells businesses that are outside its core categories or that do not meet its growth or profitability targets. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first nine months of 2003, net cash used in investing activities was $799&nbsp;million, compared with $768&nbsp;million in the first nine months of 2002. This increase primarily
reflects the absence of cash received from the 2002 divestiture of several North American food businesses, partially offset by lower purchases of businesses during the first nine months of 2003. </FONT></P>

<P ALIGN="CENTER"><FONT SIZE=2>33</FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE>
<!-- ZEQ.=2,SEQ=33,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=711204,FOLIO='33',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DW4659A.;16',USER='DINGRAM',CD='12-NOV-2003;18:19' -->
<A NAME="page_dw4659_1_34"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><U>Net
Cash Used in Financing Activities</U> </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first nine months of 2003, net cash used in financing activities was $1.7&nbsp;billion, compared with $1.5&nbsp;billion during the first nine months of 2002. The increase in net cash
used in financing activities is due primarily to higher dividend payments, an increase in Class&nbsp;A share repurchases and an increase in the net repayment of debt in 2003 (including amounts due
to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. and affiliates). </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><U>Debt
and Liquidity</U> </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Debt.</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Company's total debt, including amounts due to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. and affiliates, was $13.8&nbsp;billion at
September&nbsp;30, 2003 and $14.4&nbsp;billion at December&nbsp;31, 2002. The decrease in total debt is due primarily to the repayment of amounts due to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. and affiliates.
The Company's debt-to-equity ratio was 0.50 at September&nbsp;30, 2003 and 0.56 at December&nbsp;31, 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first quarter of 2003, the Company repaid the remaining $1.15&nbsp;billion of the 7.0% long-term notes payable to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. and affiliates. In
September&nbsp;2003, the Company issued $1.5&nbsp;billion of third-party long-term debt, including $700&nbsp;million of 5-year notes bearing interest at a rate of 4.0%
and $800&nbsp;million of 10-year notes bearing interest at 5.25%. The net proceeds from the offering were used to refinance a portion of the short-term notes payable to
Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. and affiliates. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company had short-term debt totaling $181&nbsp;million payable to Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. Interest on these
borrowings is based on the applicable London Interbank Offered Rate. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Credit Ratings.</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Following a $10.1&nbsp;billion judgment on March&nbsp;21, 2003 against Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc.'s domestic tobacco
subsidiary, Philip Morris USA&nbsp;Inc., the three major credit rating agencies took a series of ratings actions resulting in the lowering of the Company's short-term and
long-term debt ratings, despite the fact the Company is not a party to, nor has exposure to this litigation. Moody's lowered the Company's short-term debt rating from
"P-1" to "P-2" and its long-term debt rating from "A2" to "A3", with stable outlook. Standard&nbsp;&amp; Poor's lowered the Company's short-term debt
rating from "A-1" to "A-2" and its long-term debt rating from "A-" to "BBB+", with stable outlook. Fitch Rating Services lowered the Company's
short-term debt rating from "F-1" to "F-2" and its long-term debt rating from "A" to "BBB+", with stable outlook. As a result of the credit rating
agencies' actions, borrowing costs for the Company increased. None of the Company's debt agreements requires accelerated repayment in the event of a decrease in credit ratings. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Credit Lines.</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Company maintains revolving credit facilities that have historically been used to support the issuance of commercial
paper. In July&nbsp;2003, the Company's existing $3.0&nbsp;billion, 364-day revolving credit facility was terminated and replaced by a new $2.5&nbsp;billion, 364-day
revolving credit facility that expires in July&nbsp;2004. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company's credit facilities also included a $2.0&nbsp;billion, multi-year revolving credit
facility expiring in July&nbsp;2006. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, credit lines for the Company and the related activity were as follows (in billions of dollars): </FONT></P>

<A NAME="page_dw4659_1_35"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company's revolving credit facilities, which are for the sole use of the Company, require the maintenance of a minimum net worth of $18.2&nbsp;billion. The Company met this covenant at
September&nbsp;30, 2003 and expects to continue to meet this covenant. The foregoing revolving credit facilities do not include any other financial tests, any credit rating triggers or any
provisions that could require the posting of collateral. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>In
addition to the above, certain international subsidiaries of the Company maintain uncommitted credit lines to meet the short-term working capital needs of the international businesses.
These credit lines, which amounted to approximately $0.6&nbsp;billion as of September&nbsp;30, 2003, are for the sole use of the Company's international businesses. At September&nbsp;30, 2003,
borrowings on these lines amounted to approximately $0.2&nbsp;billion. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><I>Guarantees.</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As discussed in Note&nbsp;6. </FONT><FONT SIZE=2><I>Contingencies</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>, the Company had third-party
guarantees, which are primarily derived from acquisition and divestiture activities, of approximately $35&nbsp;million at September&nbsp;30, 2003. Substantially all of these guarantees expire
through 2013, with $22&nbsp;million expiring through September&nbsp;30, 2004. The Company is required to perform under these guarantees in the event that a third party fails to make contractual
payments or achieve performance measures. The Company has recorded a liability of $25&nbsp;million at September&nbsp;30, 2003 relating to these guarantees. In addition, at September&nbsp;30,
2003, the Company was contingently liable for $124&nbsp;million of guarantees related to its own performance. These include surety bonds related to dairy commodity purchases and guarantees related
to letters of credit and taxes. Guarantees do not have, and are not expected to have, a significant impact on the Company's liquidity. </FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE ALIGN="CENTER" WIDTH="120">

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company believes that its cash from operations and existing credit facilities will provide sufficient liquidity to meet its working capital needs, planned capital expenditures and payment of its
anticipated quarterly dividends. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><U>Equity
and Dividends</U> </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company repurchased 5.2&nbsp;million shares of its Class&nbsp;A common stock during the first nine months of 2003 at a cost of $156&nbsp;million. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, cumulative
repurchases under its previously announced $500&nbsp;million authority totaled 9.6&nbsp;million shares of Class&nbsp;A common stock at an aggregate cost of $326&nbsp;million. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>During
the first quarter of 2003, the Company granted shares of restricted stock and rights to receive shares of stock under its 2001 Performance Incentive Plan to eligible employees, giving them in
most instances all of the rights of stockholders, except that they may not sell, assign, pledge or otherwise encumber such shares and rights. These shares and rights are subject to forfeiture if
certain employment conditions are not met. The shares of restricted stock granted comprised approximately 3.7&nbsp;million Class&nbsp;A shares to eligible U.S.-based employees and rights to
receive approximately 1.6&nbsp;million Class&nbsp;A equivalent shares to eligible non-U.S. employees. Restrictions on the stock lapse in the first quarter of 2006. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
fair value of the restricted shares and rights at the date of grant is amortized to expense ratably over the restriction period. The Company recorded compensation expense related to restricted
stock and other stock
awards of $43&nbsp;million and $16&nbsp;million, respectively, for the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003. The unamortized portion, which is reported on the condensed
consolidated balance sheets as a reduction of earnings reinvested in the business, was $146&nbsp;million at September&nbsp;30, 2003. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Dividends
paid in the first nine months of 2003 and 2002 were $778&nbsp;million and $676&nbsp;million, respectively, an increase of 15.1%, reflecting a higher dividend rate in 2003, partially
offset by a lower number of shares outstanding as a result of share repurchases. During the third quarter of 2003, the Company's Board of Directors approved a 20% increase in the current quarterly
dividend rate to $0.18 per share on its Class&nbsp;A and Class&nbsp;B common stock. As a result, the present annualized dividend rate is $0.72 per common share. The declaration of dividends is
subject to the discretion of the Company's Board of Directors and will depend on </FONT></P>

<!-- ZEQ.=4,SEQ=35,EFW="2122198",CP="KRAFT FOODS INC",DN="1",CHK=652468,FOLIO='35',FILE='DISK022:[03CHI9.03CHI4659]DW4659A.;16',USER='DINGRAM',CD='12-NOV-2003;18:19' -->
<A NAME="page_dw4659_1_36"> </A>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>various
factors, including the Company's net earnings, financial condition, cash requirements, future prospects and other factors deemed relevant by the Company's Board of Directors. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><U>Market Risk</U>  </B></FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company operates globally, with manufacturing and sales facilities in numerous locations around the world, and utilizes certain financial instruments to manage its foreign currency and commodity
exposures, which primarily relate to forecasted transactions. Derivative financial instruments are used by the Company, principally to reduce exposures to market risks resulting from fluctuations in
foreign exchange rates and commodity prices by creating offsetting exposures. The Company is not a party to leveraged derivatives and, by policy, does not use financial instruments for speculative
purposes. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Substantially
all of the Company's derivative financial instruments are effective as hedges under Statement of Financial Accounting Standards ("SFAS") No.&nbsp;133, "Accounting for Derivative
Instruments and Hedging Activities." During the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003, ineffectiveness related to cash flow hedges was a gain of $13&nbsp;million, which was
recorded in cost of sales on the condensed consolidated statements of earnings. Ineffectiveness related to cash flow hedges during the nine months and three months ended September&nbsp;30, 2002 was
not material. At September&nbsp;30, 2003, the Company was hedging forecasted transactions for periods not exceeding fourteen months and expects substantially all amounts reported in accumulated
other comprehensive earnings (losses) to be reclassified to the consolidated statement of earnings within the next twelve months. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Foreign exchange rates.</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Company uses forward foreign exchange contracts and foreign currency options to mitigate its exposure to
changes in foreign currency exchange rates from third-party and intercompany forecasted transactions. The primary currencies to which the Company is exposed, based on the size and location of its
operations, include the euro, British pound and Canadian dollar. At September&nbsp;30, 2003 and December&nbsp;31, 2002, the Company had option and forward foreign exchange contracts with aggregate
notional amounts of $672&nbsp;million
and $575&nbsp;million, respectively, which are comprised of contracts for the purchase and sale of foreign currencies. The effective portion of unrealized gains and losses associated with forward
contracts is deferred as a component of accumulated other comprehensive earnings (losses) until the underlying hedged transactions are reported on the Company's consolidated statement of earnings. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2><B><I>Commodities.</I></B></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Company is exposed to price risk related to forecasted purchases of certain commodities used as raw materials by the
Company's businesses. Accordingly, the Company uses commodity forward contracts as cash flow hedges, primarily for coffee, cocoa, milk and cheese. Commodity futures and options are also used to hedge
the price of certain commodities, including milk, coffee, cocoa, wheat, corn, sugar and soybean oil. In general, commodity forward contracts qualify for the normal purchase exception under SFAS
No.&nbsp;133 and are, therefore, not subject to the provisions of SFAS No.&nbsp;133. At September&nbsp;30, 2003 and December&nbsp;31, 2002, the Company had net long commodity positions of
$351&nbsp;million and $544&nbsp;million, respectively. Unrealized gains or losses on net commodity positions were immaterial at September&nbsp;30, 2003 and December&nbsp;31, 2002. The
effective portion of unrealized gains and losses on commodity futures and option contracts is deferred as a component of accumulated other comprehensive earnings (losses) and is recognized as a
component of cost of sales in the Company's consolidated statement of earnings when the related inventory is sold. </FONT></P>

<HR NOSHADE ALIGN="CENTER" WIDTH="120">

<P><FONT SIZE=2>Use
of the above-mentioned derivative financial instruments has not had a material impact on the Company's financial position at September&nbsp;30, 2003 and December&nbsp;31, 2002, the Company's
results of operations for the three and nine months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003 and September&nbsp;30, 2002, or the Company's cash flows for the nine months ended September&nbsp;30, 2003 and
September&nbsp;30, 2002. </FONT></P>

<P><FONT SIZE=2>The
Company and its representatives may from time to time make written or oral forward-looking statements, including statements contained in the Company's filings with the Securities and Exchange
Commission ("SEC") and in its reports to shareholders. One can identify these forward-looking statements by use of words such as "strategy," "expects," "plans," "anticipates," "believes," "will,"

facts. These statements are based on our assumptions and estimates and are subject to risks and uncertainties. In connection with the "safe harbor" provisions of the Private Securities Litigation
Reform Act of 1995, the Company is hereby identifying important factors that could cause actual results and outcomes to differ materially from those contained in any forward-looking statement made by
or on behalf of the Company; any such statement is qualified by reference to the following cautionary statements. </FONT></P>


<P><FONT SIZE=2>Each
of the Company's segments is subject to intense competition, changes in consumer preferences, demand for its products, the effects of changing prices for its raw materials and local economic and
market conditions. Their
results are dependent upon their continued ability to promote brand equity successfully, to anticipate and respond to new consumer trends, to develop new products and markets, to broaden brand
portfolios in order to compete effectively with lower priced products in a consolidating environment at the retail and manufacturing levels, to improve productivity and to maintain access to credit
markets. The Company's results are also dependent on its ability to consummate and successfully integrate acquisitions, including its ability to derive cost savings from the integration of Nabisco's
operations with the Company. In addition, the Company is subject to the effects of foreign economies, currency movements, fluctuations in levels of customer inventories and credit and other business
risks related to its customers operating in a challenging economic and competitive environment. The Company's results are affected by its borrowing costs and credit ratings, which may in turn be
influenced by the credit ratings of Altria Group,&nbsp;Inc. The Company's benefit expense is subject to the investment performance of pension plan assets, interest rates and cost increases for
medical benefits offered to employees and retirees. The food industry continues to be subject to recalls if products become adulterated or misbranded, liability if product consumption causes injury,
ingredient disclosure and labeling laws and regulations and the possibility that consumers could lose confidence in the safety and quality of certain food products. The food industry is also subject
to recent publicity concerning the health implications of obesity and trans fatty acids. Developments in any of these areas, which are more fully described elsewhere in this document and which
descriptions are incorporated into this section by reference, could cause the Company's results to differ materially from results that have been or may be projected by or on behalf of the Company. The
Company cautions that the foregoing list of important factors is not exclusive. Any forward-looking statements are made as of the date of the document in which they appear. The Company does not
undertake to update any forward-looking statement that may be made from time to time by or on behalf of the Company. </FONT></P>

&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2><BR>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD COLSPAN=3><FONT SIZE=2><BR>
In November&nbsp;2003, the Company was advised by the Ft.&nbsp;Worth District Office of the SEC that the staff is considering recommending that the SEC bring a civil injunctive action against the Company. The notice alleges that Company employees
signed documents requested by the Fleming Companies which Fleming used in order to accelerate its revenue recognition. The notice does not contain any allegations or statements regarding the Company's accounting for its transactions with Fleming. The
Company believes that it properly recorded the transactions in accordance with generally accepted accounting principles. Prior to the time that the staff makes a formal enforcement recommendation, if any, to the SEC, the Company will have the
opportunity to respond to this notice and submit reasons why it believes the civil injunctive action should not be brought. The Company intends to respond to this notice fully and promptly and to cooperate with the SEC with respect to this matter and
the SEC's investigation of Fleming.</FONT></TD>
</TR>
<TR VALIGN="TOP">
<TD WIDTH="8%"><FONT SIZE=2><BR>
&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2><BR>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD COLSPAN=3><FONT SIZE=2><BR>
In October&nbsp;2002, Mr.&nbsp;Mustapha Gaouar filed suit in the Commercial Court of Casablanca against the Company and Mr.&nbsp;Omar Berrada claiming damages of approximately $31&nbsp;million arising from a non-compete undertaking signed by
Mr.&nbsp;Gaouar allegedly under duress. The non-compete clause was contained in an agreement concluded in 1986 between Mr.&nbsp;Gaouar and Mr.&nbsp;Berrada acting for himself and for his group of companies, including Les Cafes Ennasr (renamed Kraft
Foods Maroc), which was acquired by the Company from Mr.&nbsp;Berrada in 2001. In June&nbsp;2003, the court issued a preliminary judgment against Kraft Foods Maroc and Mr.&nbsp;Berrada holding that Mr.&nbsp;Gaouar is entitled to damages for being
deprived of the possibility of engaging in coffee roasting from 1986 due to such non-compete undertaking. At that time, the court appointed two experts to assess the amount of damages to be awarded, which assessment has not yet been completed. The
Company believes that it has various grounds for appeal of this judgment and intends to pursue such an appeal after damages have been assessed. The existence of the claims of Mr.&nbsp;Gaouar was not disclosed by Mr.&nbsp;Berrada at the time of the
Company's acquisition of Kraft Foods Maroc in 2001. As a result, in the event that the Company is ultimately found liable for damages to plaintiff in this case, the Company believes that it may have claims against Mr.&nbsp;Berrada for recovery of all
or a portion of the amount.</FONT></TD>
</TR>
<TR VALIGN="TOP">
<TD WIDTH="8%"><FONT SIZE=2><BR>
&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD WIDTH="2%"><FONT SIZE=2><BR>&nbsp;</FONT></TD>
<TD COLSPAN=3><FONT SIZE=2><BR>
In May&nbsp;2002, the Company was served with a lawsuit filed in California by the American Environmental Safety Institute against several major chocolate manufacturers alleging that the defendants' chocolate products contain "potentially dangerous
levels of lead and cadmium." The suit alleges that these levels, which are not disclosed on the product labels, are a violation of California's </FONT><FONT SIZE=2><I>Proposition 65</I></FONT><FONT SIZE=2>, which requires a warning on products
containing chemicals "known to the State" to be carcinogens or reproductive toxicants. A settlement in principle has been reached, which must be approved by the court in order to become effective. This settlement would not be financially material to
the Company.</FONT></TD>

The Company is potentially liable for certain environmental matters arising from the operations of Nabisco's former wholly-owned subsidiary, Rowe Industries. Rowe operated a small engine manufacturing facility in Sag Harbor, New York in the 1950s,
1960s and early 1970s that used various solvents. About 20 homes downgradient from the site were connected to public drinking water in the mid-1980s after solvents were detected in their individual wells. Since 1996, three toxic tort cases have been
brought against Nabisco in New York state court, collectively by or on behalf of approximately 80 individuals, including 17 minors. The first case was filed on March&nbsp;6, 1996, in the Supreme Court of the State of New York and was subsequently
dismissed by the trial court. That decision was affirmed on appeal. The other two cases both were filed on January&nbsp;3, 2000, also in the Supreme Court of the State of New York. That court granted defendant's summary judgment motion as to all but
one of the plaintiffs in each of the remaining cases, and the plaintiffs have now withdrawn their appeal of this ruling. In August&nbsp;2002, the court entered summary judgment against the remaining two plaintiffs. These two plaintiffs have appealed
this judgment, and the appeal is currently pending.</FONT></TD>

Exhibits), which contained the terms agreement and certain other documents related to its public debt offering; and (iii)&nbsp;furnished to the SEC a Current Report on Form&nbsp;8-K on October&nbsp;15, 2003, covering Item&nbsp;7 (Financial Statements
